<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Gallium</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">

				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the chemical element. <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_gallium" title="Template:Infobox gallium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_gallium" title="Template talk:Infobox gallium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_gallium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div>&#x20;|&#x20;<a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Gallium</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol&#160;<b>Ga</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a>&#160;31. It is in <a href="/wiki/Boron_group" title="Boron group">group 13</a> of the periodic table, and thus has similarities to the other metals of the group, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indium" title="Indium">indium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thallium" title="Thallium">thallium</a>. Gallium does not occur as a free element in nature, but as gallium(III) compounds in trace amounts in <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> ores and in  <a href="/wiki/Bauxite" title="Bauxite">bauxite</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Elemental gallium is a soft, silvery blue metal at <a href="/wiki/Standard_conditions_for_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard conditions for temperature and pressure">standard temperature and pressure</a>, a brittle solid at low temperatures, and a liquid at temperatures greater than 29.76&#160;°C (85.57&#160;°F) (above <a href="/wiki/Room_temperature" title="Room temperature">room temperature</a>, but below the normal human body temperature of 37.5&#160;°C (99.5&#160;°F), hence, the metal will melt in a person's hands).
</p><p>The melting point of gallium is used as a temperature reference point. Gallium alloys are used in thermometers as a non-toxic and <a href="/wiki/Environmentally_friendly" title="Environmentally friendly">environmentally friendly</a> alternative to mercury, and can withstand higher temperatures than mercury. The alloy <a href="/wiki/Galinstan" title="Galinstan">galinstan</a> (70% gallium, 21.5% <a href="/wiki/Indium" title="Indium">indium</a>, and 10% <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>) has an even lower melting point of −19&#160;°C (−2&#160;°F), well below the freezing point of water.
</p><p>Since its discovery in 1875, gallium has been used to make <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a> with low melting points. It is also used in <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductors</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Dopant" title="Dopant">dopant</a> in semiconductor substrates.
</p><p>Gallium is predominantly used in <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">electronics</a>. <a href="/wiki/Gallium_arsenide" title="Gallium arsenide">Gallium arsenide</a>, the primary <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compound</a> of gallium in electronics, is used in <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwave</a> circuits, high-speed switching circuits, and <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> circuits. Semiconducting <a href="/wiki/Gallium_nitride" title="Gallium nitride">gallium nitride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indium_gallium_nitride" title="Indium gallium nitride">indium gallium nitride</a> produce blue and violet <a href="/wiki/Light-emitting_diode" title="Light-emitting diode">light-emitting diodes</a> (LEDs) and <a href="/wiki/Diode_laser" class="mw-redirect" title="Diode laser">diode lasers</a>. Gallium is also used in the production of artificial <a href="/wiki/Gadolinium_gallium_garnet" title="Gadolinium gallium garnet">gadolinium gallium garnet</a> for jewelry.
</p><p>Gallium has no known natural role in biology. Gallium(III) behaves in a similar manner to <a href="/wiki/Ferric#Ferric_iron_and_life" title="Ferric">ferric</a> salts in biological systems and has been used in some medical applications, including pharmaceuticals and <a href="/wiki/Radiopharmacology" title="Radiopharmacology">radiopharmaceuticals</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gallium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gallium_kristallisiert.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Gallium_kristallisiert.JPG/220px-Gallium_kristallisiert.JPG" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Gallium_kristallisiert.JPG/330px-Gallium_kristallisiert.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Gallium_kristallisiert.JPG/440px-Gallium_kristallisiert.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2470" data-file-height="2018" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gallium_kristallisiert.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Crystallization of gallium from the melt</div></div></div>
<p>Elemental gallium is not found in nature, but it is easily obtained by <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelting</a>. Very pure gallium metal has a silvery color and its solid metal fractures <a href="/wiki/Conchoidal_fracture" title="Conchoidal fracture">conchoidally</a> like <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a>. Gallium liquid expands by 3.1% when it solidifies; therefore, it should not be stored in glass or metal containers because the container may rupture when the gallium changes state. Gallium shares the higher-density liquid state with a short list of other materials that includes <a href="/wiki/Properties_of_water" title="Properties of water">water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood222_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood222-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gallium <a href="/wiki/Liquid_metal_embrittlement" title="Liquid metal embrittlement">attacks</a> most other metals by <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">diffusing</a> into the metal <a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure" title="Crystal structure">lattice</a>. For example, it diffuses into the <a href="/wiki/Grain_boundary" title="Grain boundary">grain boundaries</a> of <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>-<a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> making them very brittle. Gallium easily alloys with many metals, and is used in small quantities in the <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-gallium_alloy" class="mw-redirect" title="Plutonium-gallium alloy">plutonium-gallium alloy</a> in the plutonium <a href="/wiki/Plutonium_pit" class="mw-redirect" title="Plutonium pit">cores</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear bombs</a> to stabilize the plutonium crystal structure.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">melting point</a> of gallium, at 302.9146&#160;K (29.7646&#160;°C, 85.5763&#160;°F), is just above room temperature, and is approximately the same as the average summer daytime temperatures in Earth's mid-latitudes. This melting point (mp) is one of the formal temperature reference points in the <a href="/wiki/International_Temperature_Scale_of_1990" title="International Temperature Scale of 1990">International Temperature Scale of 1990</a> (ITS-90) established by the <a href="/wiki/International_Bureau_of_Weights_and_Measures" title="International Bureau of Weights and Measures">International Bureau of Weights and Measures</a> (BIPM).<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a> of gallium, 302.9166&#160;K (29.7666&#160;°C, 85.5799&#160;°F), is used by the US <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Standards_and_Technology" title="National Institute of Standards and Technology">National Institute of Standards and Technology</a> (NIST) in preference to the melting point.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The melting point of gallium allows it to melt in the human hand, and then refreeze if removed. The liquid metal has a strong tendency to <a href="/wiki/Supercooling" title="Supercooling">supercool</a> below its <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">melting point</a>/<a href="/wiki/Freezing_point" class="mw-redirect" title="Freezing point">freezing point</a>: Ga nanoparticles can be kept in the liquid state below 90 K.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Seed_crystal" title="Seed crystal">Seeding</a> with a crystal helps to initiate freezing. Gallium is one of the four non-radioactive metals (with <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">caesium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rubidium" title="Rubidium">rubidium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>) that are known to be liquid at, or near, normal room temperature. Of the four, gallium is the only one that is neither highly reactive (rubidium and caesium) nor highly toxic (mercury) and can therefore be used in metal-in-glass high-temperature <a href="/wiki/Thermometer" title="Thermometer">thermometers</a>. It is also notable for having one of the largest liquid ranges for a metal, and for having (unlike mercury) a low <a href="/wiki/Vapor_pressure" title="Vapor pressure">vapor pressure</a> at high temperatures. Gallium's boiling point, 2673&#160;K, is more than eight times higher than its melting point on the <a href="/wiki/Absolute_scale" title="Absolute scale">absolute scale</a>, the greatest ratio between melting point and boiling point of any element.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood224_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood224-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Unlike mercury, liquid gallium metal <a href="/wiki/Wetting" title="Wetting">wets</a> glass and skin, along with most other materials (with the exceptions of quartz, graphite, and <a href="/wiki/Teflon" class="mw-redirect" title="Teflon">Teflon</a>), making it mechanically more difficult to handle even though it is substantially less toxic and requires far fewer precautions. Gallium painted onto glass is a brilliant mirror.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood221_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood221-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> For this reason as well as the metal contamination and freezing-expansion problems, samples of gallium metal are usually supplied in polyethylene packets within other containers.
</p>
<div style="float:left;margin-right:0.5em;">

<p>Gallium does not <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystallize</a> in any of the simple <a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure" title="Crystal structure">crystal structures</a>. The stable phase under normal conditions is <a href="/wiki/Orthorhombic" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthorhombic">orthorhombic</a> with 8 atoms in the conventional <a href="/wiki/Unit_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="Unit cell">unit cell</a>. Within a unit cell, each atom has only one nearest neighbor (at a distance of 244&#160;<a href="/wiki/Picometre" title="Picometre">pm</a>). The remaining six unit cell neighbors are spaced 27, 30 and 39&#160;pm farther away, and they are grouped in pairs with the same distance.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>  Many stable and <a href="/wiki/Metastability_in_molecules" class="mw-redirect" title="Metastability in molecules">metastable</a> phases are found as function of temperature and pressure.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The bonding between the two nearest neighbors is <a href="/wiki/Covalent" class="mw-redirect" title="Covalent">covalent</a>; hence Ga<sub>2</sub> <a href="/wiki/Dimer_(chemistry)" title="Dimer (chemistry)">dimers</a> are seen as the fundamental building blocks of the crystal. This explains the low melting point relative to the neighbor elements, aluminium and indium. This structure is strikingly similar to that of <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">iodine</a> and forms because of interactions between the single 4p electrons of gallium atoms, further away from the nucleus than the 4s electrons and the [Ar]3d<sup>10</sup> core. This phenomenon recurs with <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> with its "pseudo-noble-gas" [Xe]4f<sup>14</sup>5d<sup>10</sup>6s<sup>2</sup> electron configuration, which is liquid at room temperature.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood223_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood223-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The 3d<sup>10</sup> electrons do not shield the outer electrons very well from the nucleus and hence the first ionisation energy of gallium is greater than that of aluminium.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood222_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood222-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The physical properties of gallium are highly anisotropic, i.e. have different values along the three major crystallographical axes <i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, and <i>c</i> (see table), producing a significant difference between the linear (α) and volume <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">thermal expansion</a> coefficients. The properties of gallium are strongly temperature-dependent, particularly near the melting point. For example, the coefficient of thermal expansion increases by several hundred percent upon melting.<sup id="cite_ref-anis_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anis-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gallium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_gallium" title="Isotopes of gallium">Isotopes of gallium</a></div>
<p>Gallium has 31 known isotopes, ranging in <a href="/wiki/Mass_number" title="Mass number">mass number</a> from 56 to 86. Only two isotopes are stable and occur naturally, gallium-69 and gallium-71. Gallium-69 is more abundant: it makes up about 60.1% of natural gallium, while gallium-71 makes up the remaining 39.9%. All the other isotopes are radioactive. The two longest-lived and only commercially important ones are gallium-67 (half-life 3.261&#160;days) and gallium-68 (half-life 67.7&#160;min). Isotopes lighter than gallium-69 usually decay through <a href="/wiki/Beta_plus_decay" class="mw-redirect" title="Beta plus decay">beta plus decay</a> (positron emission) or <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a> to isotopes of <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>, although the lightest few (with mass numbers 56 through 59) decay through prompt <a href="/wiki/Proton_emission" title="Proton emission">proton emission</a>. Isotopes heavier than gallium-71 decay through <a href="/wiki/Beta_minus_decay" class="mw-redirect" title="Beta minus decay">beta minus decay</a> (electron emission), possibly with delayed <a href="/wiki/Neutron_emission" title="Neutron emission">neutron emission</a>, to isotopes of <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a>, while gallium-70 can decay through both beta minus decay and electron capture. Gallium-67 is unique among the light isotopes in having only electron capture as a decay mode, as its decay energy is not sufficient to allow positron emission.<sup id="cite_ref-Audi_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Audi-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_properties">Chemical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gallium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Chemical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Gallium_compounds" title="Category:Gallium compounds">Category:Gallium compounds</a>.</div>
<p>Gallium is found primarily in the +3 <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a>. The +1 oxidation state is also found in some compounds, although it is less common than it is for gallium's heavier congeners indium and thallium. For example, the very stable GaCl<sub>2</sub> contains both gallium(I) and gallium(III) and can be formulated as Ga<sup>I</sup>Ga<sup>III</sup>Cl<sub>4</sub>; in contrast, the monochloride is unstable above 0&#160;°C, <a href="/wiki/Disproportionation" title="Disproportionation">disproportionating</a> into elemental gallium and gallium(III) chloride. Compounds containing Ga–Ga bonds are true gallium(II) compounds, such as <a href="/wiki/Gallium(II)_sulfide" title="Gallium(II) sulfide">GaS</a> (which can be formulated as Ga<sub>2</sub><sup>4+</sup>(S<sup>2−</sup>)<sub>2</sub>) and the <a href="/wiki/Dioxan" class="mw-redirect" title="Dioxan">dioxan</a> complex Ga<sub>2</sub>Cl<sub>4</sub>(C<sub>4</sub>H<sub>8</sub>O<sub>2</sub>)<sub>2</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood240_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood240-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aqueous_chemistry">Aqueous chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gallium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Aqueous chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Strong acids dissolve gallium, forming gallium(III) salts such as <a href="/w/index.php?title=Gallium_sulfate&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Gallium sulfate (page does not exist)"><span class="chemf nowrap">Ga<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>(SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span></a> (gallium sulfate) and <a href="/wiki/Gallium(III)_nitrate" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallium(III) nitrate"><span class="chemf nowrap">Ga(NO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span></a> (gallium nitrate). <a href="/wiki/Aqueous" class="mw-redirect" title="Aqueous">Aqueous</a> solutions of gallium(III) salts contain the hydrated gallium ion, <span class="chemf nowrap">[Ga(H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-wiberg_holleman_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wiberg_holleman-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>1033</span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gallium(III)_hydroxide" title="Gallium(III) hydroxide">Gallium(III) hydroxide</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">Ga(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, may be precipitated from gallium(III) solutions by adding <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>. Dehydrating <span class="chemf nowrap">Ga(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> at 100&#160;°C produces gallium oxide hydroxide, GaO(OH).<sup id="cite_ref-downs_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-downs-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>140–141</span></sup>
</p><p>Alkaline <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxide</a> solutions dissolve gallium, forming <i>gallate</i> salts (not to be confused with identically-named <a href="/wiki/Gallic_acid" title="Gallic acid">gallic acid</a> salts) containing the <span class="chemf nowrap">Ga(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> anion.<sup id="cite_ref-eagleson_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eagleson-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wiberg_holleman_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wiberg_holleman-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>1033</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sipos_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sipos-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Gallium hydroxide, which is <a href="/wiki/Amphoteric" class="mw-redirect" title="Amphoteric">amphoteric</a>, also dissolves in alkali to form gallate salts.<sup id="cite_ref-downs_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-downs-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>141</span></sup> Although earlier work suggested <span class="chemf nowrap">Ga(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> as another possible gallate anion,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> it was not found in later work.<sup id="cite_ref-sipos_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sipos-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxides_and_chalcogenides">Oxides and chalcogenides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gallium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Oxides and chalcogenides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gallium reacts with the <a href="/wiki/Chalcogen" title="Chalcogen">chalcogens</a> only at relatively high temperatures. At room temperature, gallium metal is not reactive with air and water because it forms a <a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">passive</a>, protective <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxide</a> layer.  At higher temperatures, however, it reacts with atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Gallium(III)_oxide" title="Gallium(III) oxide">gallium(III) oxide</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">Ga<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-eagleson_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eagleson-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Reducing <span class="chemf nowrap">Ga<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> with elemental gallium in vacuum at 500&#160;°C to 700&#160;°C yields the dark brown <a href="/wiki/Gallium(I)_oxide" title="Gallium(I) oxide">gallium(I) oxide</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">Ga<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-downs_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-downs-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>285</span></sup> <span class="chemf nowrap">Ga<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> is a very strong <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing agent</a>, capable of reducing <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid"><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span></a> to <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide"><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S</span></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-downs_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-downs-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>207</span></sup>  It disproportionates at 800&#160;°C back to gallium and <span class="chemf nowrap">Ga<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-emeleus_sharpe_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emeleus_sharpe-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gallium(III)_sulfide" title="Gallium(III) sulfide">Gallium(III) sulfide</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">Ga<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, has 3 possible crystal modifications.<sup id="cite_ref-emeleus_sharpe_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emeleus_sharpe-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>104</span></sup> It can be made by the reaction of gallium with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S</span>) at 950&#160;°C.<sup id="cite_ref-downs_24-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-downs-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>162</span></sup> Alternatively, <span class="chemf nowrap">Ga(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> can be used at 747&#160;°C:<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 <span class="chemf nowrap">Ga(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> + 3 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S</span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">Ga<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> + 6 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span></dd></dl>
<p>Reacting a mixture of alkali metal carbonates and <span class="chemf nowrap">Ga<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> with <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S</span> leads to the formation of <i>thiogallates</i> containing the <span class="chemf nowrap">[Ga<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> anion. Strong acids decompose these salts, releasing <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S</span> in the process.<sup id="cite_ref-emeleus_sharpe_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emeleus_sharpe-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>104–105</span></sup> The mercury salt, <span class="chemf nowrap">HgGa<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>, can be used as a <a href="/wiki/Phosphor" title="Phosphor">phosphor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gallium also forms sulfides in lower oxidation states, such as <a href="/wiki/Gallium(II)_sulfide" title="Gallium(II) sulfide">gallium(II) sulfide</a> and the green <a href="/w/index.php?title=Gallium(I)_sulfide&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Gallium(I) sulfide (page does not exist)">gallium(I) sulfide</a>, the latter of which is produced from the former by heating to 1000&#160;°C under a stream of nitrogen.<sup id="cite_ref-emeleus_sharpe_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emeleus_sharpe-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>94</span></sup>
</p><p>The other binary chalcogenides, <span class="chemf nowrap">Ga<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>Se<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">Ga<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>Te<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, have the <a href="/wiki/Zincblende_(crystal_structure)" class="mw-redirect" title="Zincblende (crystal structure)">zincblende</a> structure. They are all semiconductors but are easily <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysed</a> and have limited utility.<sup id="cite_ref-emeleus_sharpe_28-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emeleus_sharpe-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>104</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nitrides_and_pnictides">Nitrides and pnictides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gallium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Nitrides and pnictides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:328px;max-width:328px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:162px;max-width:162px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Crystal-GaN.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Crystal-GaN.jpg/160px-Crystal-GaN.jpg" width="160" height="136" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Crystal-GaN.jpg/240px-Crystal-GaN.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Crystal-GaN.jpg/320px-Crystal-GaN.jpg 2x" data-file-width="389" data-file-height="331" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:162px;max-width:162px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Gallium_arsenide_crystal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Gallium_arsenide_crystal.jpg/160px-Gallium_arsenide_crystal.jpg" width="160" height="110" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Gallium_arsenide_crystal.jpg/240px-Gallium_arsenide_crystal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6f/Gallium_arsenide_crystal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="293" data-file-height="202" /></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Gallium nitride (<i>left</i>) and gallium arsenide (<i>right</i>) crystals</div></div></div>
<p>Gallium reacts with ammonia at 1050&#160;°C to form <a href="/wiki/Gallium_nitride" title="Gallium nitride">gallium nitride</a>, GaN. Gallium also forms binary compounds with <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a>: <a href="/wiki/Gallium_phosphide" title="Gallium phosphide">gallium phosphide</a> (GaP), <a href="/wiki/Gallium_arsenide" title="Gallium arsenide">gallium arsenide</a> (GaAs), and <a href="/wiki/Gallium_antimonide" title="Gallium antimonide">gallium antimonide</a> (GaSb). These compounds have the same structure as <a href="/wiki/Zinc_sulfide" title="Zinc sulfide">ZnS</a>, and have important <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconducting</a> properties.<sup id="cite_ref-wiberg_holleman_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wiberg_holleman-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>1034</span></sup> GaP, GaAs, and GaSb can be synthesized by the direct reaction of gallium with elemental phosphorus, arsenic, or antimony.<sup id="cite_ref-emeleus_sharpe_28-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emeleus_sharpe-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>99</span></sup> They exhibit higher electrical conductivity than GaN.<sup id="cite_ref-emeleus_sharpe_28-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emeleus_sharpe-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>101</span></sup> GaP can also be synthesized by reacting <span class="chemf nowrap">Ga<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> with phosphorus at low temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gallium forms ternary <a href="/wiki/Nitride" title="Nitride">nitrides</a>; for example:<sup id="cite_ref-emeleus_sharpe_28-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emeleus_sharpe-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>99</span></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">Li<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>Ga</span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">Li<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>GaN<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>Similar compounds with phosphorus and arsenic are possible: <span class="chemf nowrap">Li<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>GaP<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">Li<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>GaAs<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>. These compounds are easily hydrolyzed by dilute <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a> and water.<sup id="cite_ref-emeleus_sharpe_28-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emeleus_sharpe-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>101</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Halides">Halides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gallium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Halides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Gallium_halides" title="Gallium halides">Gallium halides</a></div>
<p>Gallium(III) oxide reacts with <a href="/wiki/Halogenation" title="Halogenation">fluorinating agents</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fluoride" title="Hydrogen fluoride">HF</a> or <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine"><span class="chemf nowrap">F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a> to form <a href="/wiki/Gallium(III)_fluoride" title="Gallium(III) fluoride">gallium(III) fluoride</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">GaF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>. It is an ionic compound strongly insoluble in water. However, it dissolves in <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid" title="Hydrofluoric acid">hydrofluoric acid</a>, in which it forms an <a href="/wiki/Adduct" title="Adduct">adduct</a> with water, <span class="chemf nowrap">GaF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>·3H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>. Attempting to dehydrate this adduct forms <span class="chemf nowrap">GaF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>OH·<i>n</i>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>. The adduct reacts with ammonia to form <span class="chemf nowrap">GaF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>·3NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, which can then be heated to form anhydrous <span class="chemf nowrap">GaF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-downs_24-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-downs-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>128–129</span></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gallium_trichloride" title="Gallium trichloride">Gallium trichloride</a> is formed by the reaction of gallium metal with <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> gas.<sup id="cite_ref-eagleson_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eagleson-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Unlike the trifluoride, gallium(III) chloride exists as dimeric molecules, <span class="chemf nowrap">Ga<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>Cl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>, with a melting point of 78&#160;°C. Eqivalent compounds are formed with bromine and iodine, <a href="/wiki/Gallium(III)_bromide" title="Gallium(III) bromide"><span class="chemf nowrap">Ga<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>Br<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span></a> and <a href="/wiki/Gallium(III)_iodide" title="Gallium(III) iodide"><span class="chemf nowrap">Ga<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>I<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-downs_24-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-downs-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>133</span></sup>
</p><p>Like the other group 13 trihalides, gallium(III) halides are <a href="/wiki/Lewis_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Lewis acid">Lewis acids</a>, reacting as halide acceptors with alkali metal halides to form salts containing <span class="chemf nowrap">GaX<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> anions, where X is a halogen. They also react with <a href="/wiki/Haloalkane" title="Haloalkane">alkyl halides</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Carbocation" title="Carbocation">carbocations</a> and <span class="chemf nowrap">GaX<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-downs_24-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-downs-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>136–137</span></sup>
</p><p>When heated to a high temperature, gallium(III) halides react with elemental gallium to form the respective gallium(I) halides. For example, <span class="chemf nowrap">GaCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> reacts with Ga to form <span class="chemf nowrap">GaCl</span>:
</p>
<dl><dd>2 Ga + <span class="chemf nowrap">GaCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> ⇌ 3 GaCl (g)</dd></dl>
<p>At lower temperatures, the equilibrium shifts toward the left and GaCl disproportionates back to elemental gallium and <span class="chemf nowrap">GaCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>. GaCl can also be produced by reacting Ga with HCl at 950&#160;°C; the product can be condensed as a red solid.<sup id="cite_ref-wiberg_holleman_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wiberg_holleman-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>1036</span></sup>
</p><p>Gallium(I) compounds can be stabilized by forming adducts with Lewis acids. For example:
</p>
<dl><dd>GaCl + <span class="chemf nowrap">AlCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">Ga<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>[AlCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>The so-called "gallium(II) halides", <span class="chemf nowrap">GaX<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, are actually adducts of gallium(I) halides with the respective gallium(III) halides, having the structure <span class="chemf nowrap">Ga<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>[GaX<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>. For example:<sup id="cite_ref-eagleson_25-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eagleson-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wiberg_holleman_23-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wiberg_holleman-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>1036</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-arora_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arora-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>GaCl + <span class="chemf nowrap">GaCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">Ga<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>[GaCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrides">Hydrides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gallium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Hydrides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Like <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>, gallium also forms a <a href="/wiki/Hydride" title="Hydride">hydride</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">GaH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Gallane" title="Gallane">gallane</a></i>, which may be produced by reacting lithium gallanate (<span class="chemf nowrap">LiGaH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>) with <a href="/wiki/Gallium(III)_chloride" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallium(III) chloride">gallium(III) chloride</a> at −30&#160;°C:<sup id="cite_ref-wiberg_holleman_23-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wiberg_holleman-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>1031</span></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>3 <span class="chemf nowrap">LiGaH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">GaCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> → 3 LiCl + 4 <span class="chemf nowrap">GaH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>In the presence of <a href="/wiki/Dimethyl_ether" title="Dimethyl ether">dimethyl ether</a> as solvent, <span class="chemf nowrap">GaH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> polymerizes to <span class="chemf nowrap">(GaH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"><i>n</i></sub></span></span>. If no solvent is used, the dimer <span class="chemf nowrap">Ga<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> (<i><a href="/wiki/Digallane" title="Digallane">digallane</a></i>) is formed as a gas. Its structure is similar to <a href="/wiki/Diborane" title="Diborane">diborane</a>, having two hydrogen atoms bridging the two gallium centers,<sup id="cite_ref-wiberg_holleman_23-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wiberg_holleman-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>1031</span></sup> unlike α-<a href="/wiki/Aluminium_hydride" title="Aluminium hydride"><span class="chemf nowrap">AlH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span></a> in which aluminium has a coordination number of 6.<sup id="cite_ref-wiberg_holleman_23-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wiberg_holleman-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>1008</span></sup>
</p><p>Gallane is unstable above −10&#160;°C, decomposing to elemental gallium and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sykes_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sykes-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organogallium_compounds">Organogallium compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gallium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Organogallium compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organogallium_chemistry" title="Organogallium chemistry">Organogallium chemistry</a></div>
<p>Organogallium compounds are of similar reactivity to organoindium compounds. less reactive than organoaluminium compounds, but more reactive than organothallium compounds.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood262_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood262-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Alkylgalliums are monomeric. <a href="/wiki/Lewis_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Lewis acid">Lewis acidity</a> decreases in the order Al &gt; Ga &gt; In and as a result organogallium compounds do not form bridged dimers as organoaluminum compounds do. Organogallium compounds are also less reactive than organoaluminum compounds. They do form stable peroxides.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> These alkylgalliums are liquids at room temperature, having low melting points, and are quite mobile and flammable. Triphenylgallium is monomeric in solution, but its crystals form chain structures due to weak intermolecluar Ga···C interactions.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood262_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood262-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gallium trichloride is a common starting reagent for the formation of organogallium compounds, such as in <a href="/wiki/Carbometalation" title="Carbometalation">carbogallation</a> reactions.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Gallium trichloride reacts with <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a> cyclopentadienide in <a href="/wiki/Diethyl_ether" title="Diethyl ether">diethyl ether</a> to form the trigonal planar gallium cyclopentadienyl complex GaCp<sub>3</sub>. Gallium(I) forms complexes with <a href="/wiki/Arene" class="mw-redirect" title="Arene">arene</a> ligands such as <a href="/wiki/Hexamethylbenzene" title="Hexamethylbenzene">hexamethylbenzene</a>. Because this ligand is quite bulky, the structure of the [Ga(η<sup>6</sup>-C<sub>6</sub>Me<sub>6</sub>)]<sup>+</sup> is that of a half-<a href="/wiki/Sandwich_compound" title="Sandwich compound">sandwich</a>. Less bulky ligands such as <a href="/wiki/Mesitylene" title="Mesitylene">mesitylene</a> allow two ligands to be attached to the central gallium atom in a bent sandwich structure. <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">Benzene</a> is even less bulky and allows the formation of dimers: an example is [Ga(η<sup>6</sup>-C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>6</sub>)<sub>2</sub>] [GaCl<sub>4</sub>]·3C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>6</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood262_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood262-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gallium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<p>In 1871, the existence of gallium was first predicted by Russian chemist <a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Mendeleev" title="Dmitri Mendeleev">Dmitri Mendeleev</a>, who named it "<a href="/wiki/Mendeleev%27s_predicted_elements" title="Mendeleev&#39;s predicted elements">eka-aluminium</a>" from its position in his <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>. He also predicted several properties of eka-aluminium that correspond closely to the real properties of gallium, such as its <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a>, <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">melting point</a>, oxide character and bonding in chloride.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><table class="wikitable">
<caption>Comparison between Mendeleev's 1871 predictions and the known properties of gallium<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood217_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood217-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
