<p>Mendeleev further predicted that eka-aluminium would be discovered by means of the <a href="/wiki/Spectroscope" class="mw-redirect" title="Spectroscope">spectroscope</a>, and that metallic eka-aluminium would dissolve slowly in both acids and alkalis and would not react with air. He also predicted that M<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> would dissolve in acids to give MX<sub>3</sub> salts, that eka-aluminium salts would form basic salts, that eka-aluminium sulfate should form <a href="/wiki/Alum" title="Alum">alums</a>, and that anhydrous MCl<sub>3</sub> should have a greater volatility than ZnCl<sub>2</sub>: all of these predictions turned out to be true.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood217_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood217-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gallium was discovered using <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">spectroscopy</a> by French chemist <a href="/wiki/Paul_Emile_Lecoq_de_Boisbaudran" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul Emile Lecoq de Boisbaudran">Paul Emile Lecoq de Boisbaudran</a> in 1875 from its characteristic spectrum (two <a href="/wiki/Violet_(color)" title="Violet (color)">violet</a> lines) in a sample of <a href="/wiki/Sphalerite" title="Sphalerite">sphalerite</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bois_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bois-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>   Later that year, Lecoq obtained the free metal by <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxide</a> in <a href="/wiki/Potassium_hydroxide" title="Potassium hydroxide">potassium hydroxide</a> solution. He named the element "gallia", from <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>Gallia</i> meaning <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a>, after his native land of France. It was later claimed that, in one of those multilingual <a href="/wiki/Pun" title="Pun">puns</a> so beloved by men of science in the 19th century, he had also named gallium after himself: "Le coq" is French for "the <a href="/wiki/Rooster" title="Rooster">rooster</a>" and the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word for "rooster" is "<i>gallus</i>".  In an 1877 article, Lecoq denied this conjecture.<sup id="cite_ref-Weeks_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Originally, de Boisbaudran determined the density of gallium as 4.7&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>, the only property that failed to match Mendeleev's predictions; Mendeleev then wrote to him and suggested that he should remeasure the density, and de Boisbaudran then obtained the correct value of 5.9&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>, that Mendeleev had predicted almost exactly.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood217_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood217-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From its discovery in 1875 until the era of semiconductors, the primary uses of gallium were high-temperature thermometrics and metal alloys with unusual properties of stability or ease of melting (some such being liquid at room temperature). The development of <a href="/wiki/Gallium_arsenide" title="Gallium arsenide">gallium arsenide</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Direct_and_indirect_band_gaps" title="Direct and indirect band gaps">direct band gap semiconductor</a> in the 1960s ushered in the most important stage in the applications of gallium.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood221_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood221-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gallium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Gallium does not exist as a free element in the Earth's crust, and the few high-content minerals, such as gallite (CuGaS<sub>2</sub>), are too rare to serve as a primary source.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The abundance <a href="/wiki/Earth#Chemical_composition" title="Earth">in the Earth's crust</a> is approximately 16.9&#160;<a href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" class="mw-redirect" title="Parts per million">ppm</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Burton_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burton-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> This is comparable to the crustal abundances of <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Niobium" title="Niobium">niobium</a>. Yet unlike these elements, gallium does not form its own ore deposits with concentrations of &gt; 0.1 wt.% in ore. Rather it occurs at trace concentrations similar to the crustal value in zinc ores,<sup id="cite_ref-:1_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> and at somewhat higher values (~ 50 ppm) in aluminium ores, from both of which it is extracted as a by-product. This lack of independent deposits is due to gallium's geochemical behaviour, showing no strong enrichment in the processes relevant to the formation of most ore deposits.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">United States Geological Survey</a> (<a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">USGS</a>) estimates that more than 1 million tons of gallium is contained in known reserves of bauxite and zinc ores.<sup id="cite_ref-USGSCS2008_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGSCS2008-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-USGSCS2006_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGSCS2006-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Some coal <a href="/wiki/Flue" title="Flue">flue</a> <a href="/wiki/Dust" title="Dust">dusts</a> contain small quantities of gallium, typically less than 1% by weight.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> However, these amounts are not extractable without mining of the host materials (see below). Thus, the availability of gallium is fundamentally determined by the rate at which bauxite, zinc ores (and coal) are extracted.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_and_availability">Production and availability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gallium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Production and availability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:6N_Gallium_sealed_in_vacuum_ampoule.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/6N_Gallium_sealed_in_vacuum_ampoule.jpg/220px-6N_Gallium_sealed_in_vacuum_ampoule.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/6N_Gallium_sealed_in_vacuum_ampoule.jpg/330px-6N_Gallium_sealed_in_vacuum_ampoule.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/6N_Gallium_sealed_in_vacuum_ampoule.jpg/440px-6N_Gallium_sealed_in_vacuum_ampoule.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3888" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:6N_Gallium_sealed_in_vacuum_ampoule.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>99.9999% (6N) gallium sealed in vacuum ampoule</div></div></div>
<p>Gallium is produced exclusively as a <a href="/wiki/By-product" title="By-product">by-product</a> during the processing of the ores of other metals. Its main source material is <a href="/wiki/Bauxite" title="Bauxite">bauxite</a>, the chief ore of <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>, but minor amounts are also extracted from sulfidic zinc ores (<a href="/wiki/Sphalerite" title="Sphalerite">sphalerite</a> being the main host mineral). In the past, certain coals were an important source.
</p><p>During the processing of bauxite to <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_oxide" title="Aluminium oxide">alumina</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Bayer_process" title="Bayer process">Bayer process</a>, gallium accumulates in the <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">sodium hydroxide</a> liquor. From this it can be extracted by a variety of methods. The most recent is the use of <a href="/wiki/Ion-exchange_resin" title="Ion-exchange resin">ion-exchange resin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Achievable extraction efficiencies critically depend on the original concentration in the feed bauxite. At a typical feed concentration of 50 ppm, about 15% of the contained gallium is extractable.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The remainder reports to the <a href="/wiki/Red_mud" title="Red mud">red mud</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_hydroxide" title="Aluminium hydroxide">aluminium hydroxide</a> streams. Gallium is removed from the ion-exchange resin in solution. Electrolysis then gives gallium metal. For <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductor</a> use, it is further purified with <a href="/wiki/Zone_melting" title="Zone melting">zone melting</a> or single-crystal extraction from a melt (<a href="/wiki/Czochralski_process" title="Czochralski process">Czochralski process</a>). Purities of 99.9999% are routinely achieved and commercially available.<sup id="cite_ref-Moskalyk_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moskalyk-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bauxite_Jamaica_1984.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Bauxite_Jamaica_1984.jpg/220px-Bauxite_Jamaica_1984.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Bauxite_Jamaica_1984.jpg/330px-Bauxite_Jamaica_1984.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Bauxite_Jamaica_1984.jpg/440px-Bauxite_Jamaica_1984.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2012" data-file-height="1324" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bauxite_Jamaica_1984.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bauxite mine in <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a> (1984).</div></div></div>
<p>Its by-product status means that gallium production is constrained by the amount of bauxite, sulfidic zinc ores (and coal) extracted per year. Therefore, its availability needs to be discussed in terms of supply potential. The supply potential of a by-product is defined as that amount which is economically extractable from its host materials <i>per year</i> under current market conditions (i.e. technology and price).<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Reserves and resources are not relevant for by-products, since they <i>cannot</i> be extracted independently from the main-products.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Recent estimates put the supply potential of gallium at a minimum of 2,100 t/yr from bauxite, 85 t/yr from sulfidic zinc ores, and potentially 590 t/yr from coal.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> These figures are significantly greater than current production (375 t in 2016).<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Thus, major future increases in the by-product production of gallium will be possible without significant increases in production costs or price. The average gallium price in 2015 was $US317/kg, down from $US688/kg in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gallium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Semiconductor applications dominate the commercial demand for gallium, accounting for 98% of the total.  The next major application is for <a href="/wiki/Gadolinium_gallium_garnet" title="Gadolinium gallium garnet">gadolinium gallium garnets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Semiconductors">Semiconductors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gallium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Semiconductors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Blue_LED_and_Reflection.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Blue_LED_and_Reflection.jpg/220px-Blue_LED_and_Reflection.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Blue_LED_and_Reflection.jpg/330px-Blue_LED_and_Reflection.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Blue_LED_and_Reflection.jpg/440px-Blue_LED_and_Reflection.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Blue_LED_and_Reflection.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gallium-based blue LEDs</div></div></div>
<p>Extremely high-purity (&gt;99.9999%) gallium is commercially available to serve the semiconductor industry. <a href="/wiki/Gallium_arsenide" title="Gallium arsenide">Gallium arsenide</a> (GaAs) and <a href="/wiki/Gallium_nitride" title="Gallium nitride">gallium nitride</a> (GaN) used in electronic components represented about 98% of the gallium consumption in the United States in 2007. About 66% of semiconductor gallium is used in the U.S. in integrated circuits (mostly gallium arsenide), such as the manufacture of ultra-high-speed logic chips and <a href="/wiki/MESFET" title="MESFET">MESFETs</a> for low-noise microwave preamplifiers in cell phones. About 20% of this gallium is used in <a href="/wiki/Optoelectronic" class="mw-redirect" title="Optoelectronic">optoelectronics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-USGSCS2008_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGSCS2008-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Worldwide, gallium arsenide makes up 95% of the annual global gallium consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-Moskalyk_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moskalyk-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gallium arsenide is used in a variety of optoelectronic infrared devices. <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_gallium_arsenide" title="Aluminium gallium arsenide">Aluminium gallium arsenide</a> (AlGaAs) is used in high-power infrared laser diodes. The semiconductors gallium nitride and <a href="/wiki/Indium_gallium_nitride" title="Indium gallium nitride">indium gallium nitride</a> are used in blue and violet optoelectronic devices, mostly <a href="/wiki/Laser_diode" title="Laser diode">laser diodes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Light-emitting_diode" title="Light-emitting diode">light-emitting diodes</a>. For example, gallium nitride 405&#160;nm diode lasers are used as a violet light source for higher-density <a href="/wiki/Blu-ray_Disc" class="mw-redirect" title="Blu-ray Disc">Blu-ray Disc</a> compact data disc drives.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Multijunction_photovoltaic_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="Multijunction photovoltaic cell">Multijunction photovoltaic cells</a>, developed for <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">satellite</a> power applications, are made by <a href="/wiki/Molecular-beam_epitaxy" title="Molecular-beam epitaxy">molecular-beam epitaxy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Metalorganic_vapour-phase_epitaxy" title="Metalorganic vapour-phase epitaxy">metalorganic vapour-phase epitaxy</a> of <a href="/wiki/Thin_film" title="Thin film">thin films</a> of gallium arsenide, <a href="/wiki/Indium_gallium_phosphide" title="Indium gallium phosphide">indium gallium phosphide</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Indium_gallium_arsenide" title="Indium gallium arsenide">indium gallium arsenide</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Mars_Exploration_Rover" title="Mars Exploration Rover">Mars Exploration Rovers</a> and several satellites use triple-junction gallium arsenide on germanium cells.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Gallium is also a component in <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Photovoltaic">photovoltaic</a> compounds (such as copper indium gallium selenium sulfide Cu(In,Ga)(Se,S)<sub>2</sub>) used in solar panels as a cost-efficient alternative to <a href="/wiki/Crystalline_silicon" title="Crystalline silicon">crystalline silicon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Galinstan_and_other_alloys">Galinstan and other alloys</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gallium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Galinstan and other alloys">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Galinstan_on_glass.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Galinstan_on_glass.jpg/220px-Galinstan_on_glass.jpg" width="220" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Galinstan_on_glass.jpg/330px-Galinstan_on_glass.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Galinstan_on_glass.jpg/440px-Galinstan_on_glass.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Galinstan_on_glass.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Galinstan from a broken thermometer, easily wetting a piece of glass.</div></div></div>
<p>Gallium readily <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a> with most metals, and is used as an ingredient in <a href="/wiki/Low-melting_alloy" class="mw-redirect" title="Low-melting alloy">low-melting alloys</a>. The nearly <a href="/wiki/Eutectic" class="mw-redirect" title="Eutectic">eutectic</a> alloy of gallium, <a href="/wiki/Indium" title="Indium">indium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> is a room temperature liquid used in medical thermometers. This alloy, with the trade-name <i><a href="/wiki/Galinstan" title="Galinstan">Galinstan</a></i> (with the "-stan" referring to the tin, <i>stannum</i> in Latin), has a low freezing point of −19&#160;°C (−2.2&#160;°F).<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> It has been suggested that this family of alloys could also be used to cool computer chips in place of water.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Gallium alloys have been evaluated as substitutes for mercury <a href="/wiki/Dental_amalgam" class="mw-redirect" title="Dental amalgam">dental amalgams</a>, but these materials have yet to see wide acceptance.
</p><p>Because gallium <a href="/wiki/Wetting" title="Wetting">wets</a> glass or <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a>, gallium can be used to create brilliant <a href="/wiki/Mirror" title="Mirror">mirrors</a>. When the wetting action of gallium-alloys is not desired (as in <a href="/wiki/Galinstan" title="Galinstan">Galinstan</a> glass thermometers), the glass must be protected with a transparent layer of <a href="/wiki/Gallium(III)_oxide" title="Gallium(III) oxide">gallium(III) oxide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> used in <a href="/wiki/Plutonium_pit" class="mw-redirect" title="Plutonium pit">nuclear weapon pits</a> is stabilized in the <a href="/wiki/Allotropes_of_plutonium" title="Allotropes of plutonium">δ phase</a> and made machinable by <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-gallium_alloy" class="mw-redirect" title="Plutonium-gallium alloy">alloying with gallium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biomedical_applications">Biomedical applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gallium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Biomedical applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although gallium has no natural function in biology, gallium ions interact with processes in the body in a manner similar to <a href="/wiki/Ferric#Ferric_iron_and_life" title="Ferric">iron(III)</a>. Because these processes include <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a>, a marker for many disease states, several gallium salts are used (or are in development) as <a href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical_drug" class="mw-redirect" title="Pharmaceutical drug">pharmaceuticals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radiopharmacology" title="Radiopharmacology">radiopharmaceuticals</a> in medicine. Interest in the anticancer properties of gallium emerged when it was discovered that <sup>67</sup>Ga(III) citrate injected in tumor-bearing animals localized to sites of tumor. Clinical trials have shown gallium nitrate to have antineoplastic activity against non-Hodgkin’s lymphoma and urothelial cancers. A new generation of gallium-ligand complexes such as tris(8-quinolinolato)gallium(III) (KP46)
and gallium maltolate has emerged.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gallium_nitrate" title="Gallium nitrate">Gallium nitrate</a> (brand name Ganite) has been used as an intravenous pharmaceutical to treat <a href="/wiki/Hypercalcemia" class="mw-redirect" title="Hypercalcemia">hypercalcemia</a> associated with tumor <a href="/wiki/Metastasis" title="Metastasis">metastasis</a> to bones. Gallium is thought to interfere with <a href="/wiki/Osteoclast" title="Osteoclast">osteoclast</a> function, and the therapy may be effective when other treatments have failed.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gallium_maltolate" title="Gallium maltolate">Gallium maltolate</a>, an oral, highly absorbable form of gallium(III) ion, is an anti-proliferative to pathologically proliferating cells, particularly cancer cells and some bacteria that accept it in place of ferric iron (Fe<sup>3+</sup>). Researchers are conducting clinical and preclinical trials on this compound as a potential treatment for a number of cancers, infectious diseases, and inflammatory diseases.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When gallium ions are mistakenly taken up in place of iron(III) by bacteria such as <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudomonas" title="Pseudomonas">Pseudomonas</a></i>, the ions interfere with respiration, and the bacteria die. This happens because iron is redox-active, allowing the transfer of electrons during respiration, while gallium is redox-inactive.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A complex <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amine</a>-<a href="/wiki/Phenol" title="Phenol">phenol</a> Ga(III) compound MR045 is selectively toxic to parasites resistant to <a href="/wiki/Chloroquine" title="Chloroquine">chloroquine</a>, a common drug against <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>. Both the Ga(III) complex and chloroquine act by inhibiting crystallization of <a href="/wiki/Hemozoin" title="Hemozoin">hemozoin</a>, a disposal product formed from the digestion of blood by the parasites.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiogallium_salts">Radiogallium salts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gallium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Radiogallium salts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gallium-67" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallium-67">Gallium-67</a> <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a> such as gallium <a href="/wiki/Citrate" class="mw-redirect" title="Citrate">citrate</a> and gallium <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrate</a> are used as <a href="/wiki/Radiopharmaceutical" title="Radiopharmaceutical">radiopharmaceutical</a> agents in the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_medicine" title="Nuclear medicine">nuclear medicine</a> imaging known as <a href="/wiki/Gallium_67_scan" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallium 67 scan">gallium scan</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Radionuclide" title="Radionuclide">radioactive isotope</a> <sup>67</sup>Ga is used, and the compound or salt of gallium is unimportant. The body handles Ga<sup>3+</sup> in many ways as though it were Fe<sup>3+</sup>, and the ion is bound (and concentrates) in areas of inflammation, such as infection, and in areas of rapid cell division. This allows such sites to be imaged by nuclear scan techniques.<sup id="cite_ref-Nordberg_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nordberg-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gallium-68" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallium-68">Gallium-68</a>, a positron emitter with a half-life of 68 min, is now used as a diagnostic radionuclide in PET-CT when linked to pharmaceutical preparations such as <a href="/wiki/DOTATOC" class="mw-redirect" title="DOTATOC">DOTATOC</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Somatostatin" title="Somatostatin">somatostatin</a> analogue used for <a href="/wiki/Neuroendocrine_tumors" class="mw-redirect" title="Neuroendocrine tumors">neuroendocrine tumors</a> investigation, and <a href="/wiki/DOTA-TATE" title="DOTA-TATE">DOTA-TATE</a>, a newer one, used for neuroendocrine <a href="/wiki/Metastasis" title="Metastasis">metastasis</a> and lung neuroendocrine cancer, such as certain types of <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microcytoma&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Microcytoma (page does not exist)">microcytoma</a></i>. Gallium-68's preparation as a pharmaceutical is chemical, and the radionuclide is extracted by <a href="/wiki/Elution" title="Elution">elution</a> from germanium-68, a <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_radioisotope" title="Synthetic radioisotope">synthetic radioisotope</a> of <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Gallium-68_generator" title="Gallium-68 generator">gallium-68 generators</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_uses">Other uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gallium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Other uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gallium is used for <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrino</a> detection. Possibly the largest amount of pure gallium ever collected in a single spot is the Gallium-Germanium Neutrino Telescope used by the <a href="/wiki/SAGE_(Soviet-American_Gallium_Experiment)" class="mw-redirect" title="SAGE (Soviet-American Gallium Experiment)">SAGE</a> experiment at the Baksan Neutrino Observatory in Russia. This detector contains 55–57 tonnes of liquid gallium.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Another experiment was the <a href="/wiki/GALLEX" title="GALLEX">GALLEX</a> neutrino detector operated in the early 1990s in an Italian mountain tunnel. The detector contained 12.2 tons of watered gallium-71. Solar neutrinos caused a few atoms of <sup>71</sup>Ga to become radioactive <sup>71</sup><a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">Ge</a>, which were detected. This experiment showed that the solar neutrino flux is 40% less than theory predicted. This deficit was not explained until better solar neutrino detectors and theories were constructed (see <a href="/wiki/Sudbury_Neutrino_Observatory" title="Sudbury Neutrino Observatory">SNO</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gallium is also used as a <a href="/wiki/Liquid_metal_ion_source" title="Liquid metal ion source">liquid metal ion source</a> for a <a href="/wiki/Focused_ion_beam" title="Focused ion beam">focused ion beam</a>. For example, a focused gallium-ion beam was used to create the world's smallest book, <i><a href="/wiki/Teeny_Ted_from_Turnip_Town" title="Teeny Ted from Turnip Town">Teeny Ted from Turnip Town</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-pr_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pr-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Another use of gallium is as an additive in glide wax for skis, and other low-friction surface materials.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A well-known <a href="/wiki/Practical_joke" title="Practical joke">practical joke</a> among chemists is to fashion gallium spoons and use them to serve tea to unsuspecting guests, since gallium has a similar appearance to its lighter homolog aluminium. The spoons then melt in the hot tea.<sup id="cite_ref-Sam_Kean2010_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sam_Kean2010-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precautions">Precautions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gallium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Precautions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Metallic gallium is not toxic. However, exposure to gallium halide complexes can result in acute toxicity.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The Ga<sup>3+</sup> ion of soluble gallium salts tends to form the insoluble hydroxide when injected in large doses; precipitation of this hydroxide resulted in <a href="/wiki/Renal" class="mw-redirect" title="Renal">renal</a> toxicity in animals. In lower doses, soluble gallium is tolerated well and does not accumulate as a poison, instead being excreted mostly through urine. Excretion of gallium occurs in two phases: the first phase has a <a href="/wiki/Biological_half-life" title="Biological half-life">biological half-life</a> of 1 hour, while the second has a biological half-life of 25 hours.<sup id="cite_ref-Nordberg_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nordberg-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gallium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
